The manual focuses on high-level usage rather than advanced window management rules, treating that as delegated to upstream Hyprland documentation.

Based on your Hyprland config, here are both options:

## Option 1: Separate Workspace (Recommended - Much Easier)

Add this to the bottom of your `~/.config/hypr/hyprland.conf`:

```
# Discord on workspace 10
windowrule = workspace 10, class:discord
```

This will always send Discord to workspace 10. You can switch there with `Super+0`.

## Option 2: Background on Workspace 1 (Complex)

True "background" behavior is tricky in Hyprland since windows don't have z-order layers like traditional desktops. You could try:

```
# Discord fullscreen on workspace 1
windowrule = workspace 1, class:discord
windowrule = fullscreen, class:discord
```

But the problem is: other windows on workspace 1 would be **behind** fullscreen Discord, not in front of it. There's no native way to pin a window to the background layer.

---

**My recommendation**: Use the separate workspace approach. It's cleaner and you can quickly peek at Discord with `Super+0`, then return with `Super+1`. You could also use a scratchpad/special workspace if you want Discord to overlay temporarily.

Would you like me to add the workspace rule to your config? And do you want it on workspace 10, or a different one?

> Let's do Discord on workspace 3.

> How can we launch it as a native app? Is there a difference?

**Differences:**

| Web App | Native App |
|---------|------------|
| Runs in Chrome/browser wrapper | Standalone Electron app |
| Shares browser resources | Has its own process/memory |
| No system tray integration | System tray, better notifications |
| Limited push-to-talk support | Full push-to-talk (global hotkeys) |
| No game activity detection | Can detect what game you're playing |
| Lighter weight | Heavier but more features |

**For voice chat and gaming**, the native app is better. For just text chat, the web app works fine.

To install native Discord on Arch:

```bash
yay -S discord
```

Or if you prefer the Flatpak version:

```bash
flatpak install flathub com.discordapp.Discord
```

Want me to install the native version? If so, which do you prefer - the AUR package (`discord`) or Flatpak?

> It says there's a new update and telling me to choose between a .deb and .tar.gz file, what do I do?

Skip that dialog - on Arch you don't use those files. Discord updates through the package manager instead.

Close the dialog and run:

```bash
yay -S discord
```

This will reinstall/update Discord from the AUR with the latest version. The in-app updater is meant for Debian (.deb) and manual installs (.tar.gz), not Arch.

> I ignore the prompt but then nothing happens.

Discord is blocking until you update. Let's update it through the package manager:

⏺ Bash (yay -S discord --noconfirm)

Discord says it's already at the latest version in the Arch repos (0.0.118), but Discord's servers want a newer version. This happens when Discord updates faster than the package maintainers.

Options:

1. **Try launching again** - sometimes Discord accepts the version after a reinstall

2. **Use Flatpak instead** (updates independently from Arch repos):
   ```bash
   sudo pacman -R discord
   flatpak install flathub com.discordapp.Discord
   ```
   Then I'd update your keybinding to use the Flatpak.

3. **Wait** - the Arch package usually catches up within a day or two

Want to try launching it again first, or switch to Flatpak?

Now I have the full picture. Here's the comparison:

---

## Your Old Config (vim-plug, Windows)

**Plugin Manager:** vim-plug  
**Leader:** `,`

**Plugins:**
| Plugin | Purpose |
|--------|---------|
| treesitter | Syntax highlighting |
| nvim-tree | File explorer |
| lualine + bufferline | Status bar + tabs |
| catppuccin | Theme |
| hop.nvim | Jump to words (like flash/leap) |
| nvim-snippy | Snippets |
| Comment.nvim | Toggle comments |
| ctrlp | Fuzzy file finder |
| markdown-preview | Preview markdown |
| smartcolumn | Column guide |
| indent-guides | Show indentation |
| moonscript/yuescript | Language support |

**Key Settings:**
- Relative line numbers
- 2-space tabs, expandtab
- No swap/backup files
- Persistent undo
- Block cursor always
- Show EOL markers (¬)
- Autochdir (cd to current file's dir)

**Custom Keymaps:**
| Key | Action |
|-----|--------|
| `,w` | Save |
| `,q` | Quit |
| `,f` | File finder (CtrlP) |
| `H` / `L` | Line start / end |
| `s` | Hop (jump to word) |
| `Ctrl+h/l` | Prev/next buffer |
| `Ctrl+w` | Close buffer |
| `Alt+j/k` | Move lines up/down (visual) |
| `Space` | Toggle fold |
| `S` | Search/replace word under cursor |
| `,gc` / `,gb` | Comment line/block |
| `F2` | Open file tree |

---

## Current Omarchy Config (LazyVim)

**Plugin Manager:** lazy.nvim  
**Leader:** `Space` (LazyVim default)

LazyVim **already includes** equivalents for most of your plugins:
- **Treesitter** ✓
- **File explorer**: neo-tree (instead of nvim-tree)
- **Status bar**: lualine ✓
- **Bufferline**: bufferline ✓
- **Jump**: flash.nvim (instead of hop)
- **Comments**: built-in
- **Fuzzy finder**: Telescope (instead of ctrlp)
- **Snippets**: LuaSnip
- **LSP, formatting, linting**: all preconfigured

**Current customizations:**
- Relative numbers disabled

---

## Recommendations

**Keep from Omarchy/LazyVim:**
- lazy.nvim (better plugin manager)
- Telescope (superior to ctrlp)
- flash.nvim (modern hop replacement)
- neo-tree (works well)
- All the LSP/formatting setup
- The theme system (syncs with Omarchy)

**Port from your old config:**

1. **Options** (add to `options.lua`):
```lua
vim.opt.showmatch = true
vim.opt.matchtime = 2
vim.opt.swapfile = false
vim.opt.writebackup = false
vim.opt.listchars = 'eol:¬'
vim.opt.list = true
vim.opt.shiftwidth = 2
vim.opt.tabstop = 2
vim.opt.guicursor = 'a:block-Cursor/lCursor'
vim.opt.relativenumber = true  -- you had this, currently disabled
```

2. **Keymaps** (add to `keymaps.lua`):
```lua
vim.keymap.set({'n', 'v'}, 'L', '$')
vim.keymap.set({'n', 'v'}, 'H', '^')
vim.keymap.set('v', '<A-j>', ":m '>+1<CR>gv=gv")
vim.keymap.set('v', '<A-k>', ":m '<-2<CR>gv=gv")
```

3. **Maybe add:**
- yuescript/moonscript plugins if you still use those languages
- markdown-preview if you need live preview

---

**Don't port:**
- vim-plug setup (lazy.nvim is better)
- ctrlp (Telescope is better, use `<leader>ff`)
- hop (flash.nvim is already there, use `s`)
- Windows-specific paths
- autochdir (can conflict with project-based workflows)

Yes, **flash.nvim shows labels everywhere on screen** by default. Looking at the config:

```lua
multi_window = true,  -- searches in ALL windows
forward = true,
wrap = true,          -- wraps around, so shows both directions
```

**How it works:**
1. Press `s`
2. Type a character or two (e.g., `fo`)
3. All matches across the entire visible area get labels (both above and below cursor)
4. Press the label letter to jump

The main difference from hop.nvim:
- **hop**: Press `s`, immediately see labels on ALL words
- **flash**: Press `s`, type a few chars, THEN see labels on matches

If you prefer hop's behavior (instant labels without typing first), you can still use hop. Want me to add hop.nvim alongside flash, or does flash's approach work for you?

> I prefer hop's because it's faster, but I'll try flash to see what it's like in practice. You may do your recommended changes, if I feel anything else is missing we can just fix it later.

**Options** (`options.lua`):
- Relative line numbers
- Show matching brackets (with 0.2s highlight)
- No swap/backup files
- EOL markers (¬)

**Keymaps** (`keymaps.lua`):

Options:
1. **Override anyway** - use `Super+V` for NeoVim, lose universal paste
2. **Use `Super+N`** - for **N**eovim (if available)
3. **Use `Super+E`** - common for **E**ditor

Which do you prefer?

⏺ Bash (yay -Ss ^dropbox$ 2>/dev/null || yay -Ss dropbox | grep -E "...)  
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;⎿ Result:

```
omarchy/dropbox 238.4.6075-1 (66.0 MiB 113.6 MiB) 
    A free service that lets you bring your photos, docs, and videos anywhere and share them easily.

Dropbox is available. Run this in your terminal:

```bash
yay -S dropbox
```

After installation, start Dropbox and it will open a browser for login. Once logged in, you can set the folder location:

1. Click the Dropbox tray icon → Preferences
2. Go to "Sync" tab
3. Click "Move..." next to Dropbox folder location
4. Select your home folder and name it `dropbox`

Or before first sync, you can create the symlink preemptively:
```bash
mkdir -p ~/dropbox
ln -s ~/dropbox ~/.dropbox-dist/../Dropbox
```

Let me know once you've installed it and I can help with the folder setup.

> It's installed.

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;⎿ Result:

```
Exit code 2
```

Dropbox hasn't run yet. Start it:

```bash
dropbox &
```

It will open a browser for login. During setup, when it asks where to put the Dropbox folder, click **"Advanced Settings"** or similar and choose:
- Location: `/home/adn`
- Folder name: `dropbox`

Let me know when you've logged in and set it up.

> I logged in with the wrong Google account by accident, how do I get the browser popup to show again?

Stop Dropbox and delete its config to start fresh:

```bash
pkill dropbox
rm -rf ~/.dropbox ~/.dropbox-dist
dropbox &
```

This will show the login popup again.

> OK, it says "Dropbox can't load the libappindicator library required for the tray icon."

Install the missing library:

```bash
yay -S libappindicator-gtk3
```

Then restart Dropbox:

```bash
pkill dropbox
dropbox &
```

> There was no popup with Advanced Settings, now it's syncing to Dropbox, not dropbox

No problem, let's fix it:

```bash
pkill dropbox
mv ~/Dropbox ~/dropbox
ln -s ~/dropbox ~/Dropbox
dropbox &
```

This moves the folder to `dropbox` and creates a symlink so Dropbox still finds it.

> Should I pause syncing? This won't change anything on the server files that Dropbox holds right?

No, this won't change anything on Dropbox's servers. We're just renaming the local folder and creating a symlink pointing to it - Dropbox will still see all files in the same place.

But yes, pausing or killing Dropbox first is safer to avoid any sync conflicts during the move. The `pkill dropbox` command stops it completely, so just run those commands in order and you're good.

> Is there no way to make dropbox directly use dropbox without the symlink?

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;⎿ Result:

/home/adn/dropbox:
total 16
drwx------ 1 adn adn  52 Dec 31 18:27 .
{"personal": {"path": "/home/adn/Dropbox", "host": 19609391603, "is_team": false, "subscription_type": "Basic"}}
```

The symlink is already there. To make Dropbox use `dropbox` directly:

```bash
pkill dropbox
rm ~/Dropbox
sed -i 's|/home/adn/Dropbox|/home/adn/dropbox|g' ~/.dropbox/info.json
dropbox &
```

This updates Dropbox's config to point to `dropbox` directly and removes the symlink.

> The Dropbox folder already has some of my files synced. Will removing it not removing it on the server?

No worries - `~/Dropbox` is just a symlink (a pointer) to `~/dropbox`. Your actual files are in `~/dropbox`.

Running `rm ~/Dropbox` only removes the symlink, not the folder it points to. Your files in `~/dropbox` stay untouched, and nothing changes on the server.

Click **"Relink"** - it will reconnect to your account using the new `dropbox` path we set in the config. Your files are still there and it will continue syncing normally.

```
1→# Anchor Implementation Plan
     2→
     3→C-first approach with YueScript as the scripting layer. Minimize plain Lua; write game-facing code in YueScript from the start.
     4→
     5→**Target Game:** Emoji Ball Battles — Minesweeper dungeon exploration + physics auto-battler combat.
     6→
     7→---
     8→
     9→## Build Strategy
    10→
    11→### YueScript Compilation
    12→
    13→**Decision: Build-time compilation**
    14→
    15→Compile `.yue` → `.lua` during build, not at runtime. This:
    16→- Eliminates runtime dependency on YueScript compiler
    17→- Produces debuggable `.lua` files for error inspection
    18→- Simplifies the C side (just loads standard Lua)
    19→- Can add runtime compilation later for hot reloading
    20→
    21→**Build flow:**
    22→```
    23→game.yue ──► yue -r ──► game.lua ──► embedded in executable / loaded at runtime
    24→                ↑
    25→                └── -r flag preserves line numbers for debugging
    26→```
    27→
    28→**Directory structure:**
    29→```
    30→anchor/
... [697 more lines]

1→# Anchor Engine Specification

---

## Summary

**The Project:** You're building **Anchor**, a game engine in C with YueScript/Lua scripting, and using it to make **Emoji Ball Battles** — a physics auto-battler roguelite with Minesweeper-style dungeon navigation.

### What Anchor Is

A minimalist 2D engine with:
- **C backend**: SDL2 for window/input/audio, software rendering, Box2D 3.1 for physics
- **YueScript frontend**: All game logic in YueScript (MoonScript variant) compiling to Lua
- **Object tree**: Parent-child hierarchy where children die with parents, automatic cleanup
- **Operator syntax**: Concise DSL (`E()`, `^`, `/`, `+`, `>>`) for object construction
- **Springs/Timers**: Built-in "juice" primitives for game feel

### The Game: Emoji Ball Battles

- **Dungeon phase**: Navigate a Minesweeper grid, avoid mines, find the boss
- **Combat phase**: Physics-based auto-battler where emoji balls collide and damage each other
- **Roguelite loop**: Survive floors, collect upgrades, grow stronger

### How We'll Work

1. **Incremental steps** — small pieces, show/test, get feedback, then proceed
2. **No autonomous juice** — you have specific taste; I ask or leave mechanical
3. **C for low-level, YueScript for logic** — only move to C when profiling shows need
4. **Locality** — code should be readable in one place, no scattered systems
5. **Documentation** — keep ANCHOR.md and CLAUDE.md updated as we implement

### Current State

The engine is **not yet implemented**. The .yue files are example game code showing how the API *will* work once we build the C foundation. The implementation plan has 8 phases:

1. C Skeleton + Web Build
2. Rendering (software, layers, effects)
3. Input
4. Audio
5. Physics (Box2D 3.1)
6. Random
6.5. Text Rendering
7. YueScript Object System
8. Emoji Ball Battles (the actual game)

---

Ready to start building when you are.

The same applies to implementation plans and so on, if we change plans, if we check a certain task off, the documents should have that clear always. Third, the logs generated from the session, along with a summary of what was done (generated by you), should be uploaded to the logs folder in the dropbox folder, see the home.md file in the pages folder of the Anchor folder there for an explanation of why. One test I run with AI models to see if they're good is trying to get them to code like me. I upload a bunch of prototypes I wrote in the past, give them my engine's documentation, and tell them to try to code something new.

In the past, they all failed. I couldn't tell if it was because of poor reasoning skills, or too small context, but they just couldn't do it. This changed last month (November 2025) with the release of Opus 4.5.

This is the first time a model has been able to succeed at this task, and now I have the computer coding exactly like I do, not only using my engine's API correctly, but also following my coding style and my coding *philosophy,* which has some fairly high-level "soft" concepts to it.

This is, functionally, my "AGI is here" moment. As I was finishing [It Follows](https://a327ex.com/posts/it_follows) during December, I spent maybe 3-4 days in total talking to Opus to assess its capabilities. I was pleasantly surprised. It understands your intent, catches its own mistakes, and has genuine taste.

Other models can do some of this, but something always feels off, like they're too literal, or subtly bullshitting you. Opus instead is... *coherent?* Everything works together and it feels like talking to someone who actually understands, it just thinks correctly.

And so my conversations with it generated these documents (view them with formatting on the sidebar to the left):

[Anchor Engine Specification](/files/Opus 4.5/ANCHOR.md)  
[CLAUDE.md](/files/Opus 4.5/CLAUDE.md)  
[Anchor Implementation Plan](/files/Opus 4.5/IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md)  

This is the specification of my engine and how Claude should work with it, but also a plan to implement it in C, which is something I've been meaning to do for a long time to move away from LÖVE, but never got around to.

We also made quite a few improvements to the engine's API that I likely wouldn't have been able to do by myself. My favorite was telling it to read [Amulet's docs](https://www.amulet.xyz/doc/) and asking how we could steal the operator syntax it uses for its scene graph.

It did so and proposed the operator syntax I ended up using for inline anonymous object creation, with symbols such as `^`, `/`, `>>`:

```yuescript
spawn_particle = (x, y, color, vx, vy) ->
  E! ^ {x: x, y: y, color: color, radius: 3}
     ^ =>
... [23 more lines]
```

## Detailed Plan for Session Workflow

### The Challenge

Claude Code hooks run shell commands *after* I'm done responding, but at that point I can't generate new content. So we need a **two-part approach**:

1. **Manual end-of-session ritual** — You tell me to wrap up, I do all the work
2. **Hooks** — For logging timestamps and ensuring nothing is missed

---

## Part 1: File Structure

### Project Directory (`/home/adn/a327ex/`)

```
a327ex/
├── CLAUDE.md              ← Instructions for ALL Claude instances
├── ANCHOR.md              ← Engine spec (I update this)
├── IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md ← Checklist (I update this)
├── .claude/
│   ├── settings.json      ← Hook configuration
│   └── hooks/
│       └── session-end.sh ← Backup logging script
├── src/                   ← (future) C source
├── yue/                   ← (future) YueScript source
└── ...
```

### Dropbox Website (`/home/adn/dropbox/Apps/Blot/Anchor/`)

```
Anchor/
├── context/               ← Copies of ANCHOR.md, CLAUDE.md, etc. (for website)
├── logs/                  ← Session logs (YYYY-MM-DD-N.md)
├── pages/                 ← Website pages
├── files/                 ← Downloadable files
└── notes/                 ← Notes
```

### GitHub Repos (to create)

1. **`a327ex/anchor`** — Main project repo
2. **`a327ex/anchor-website`** — Blot website repo (or could be same repo, different branch)

## Part 2: The CLAUDE.md Workflow Section

I'll add a section to CLAUDE.md that every future Claude instance will read. It will instruct them to:

1. **At session start**: Review what's pending in IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md
2. **During session**: Update docs as APIs/plans change
3. **At session end**: Run the end-of-session ritual when asked

The key is a **specific command** you can say:

> "Let's wrap up this session" or "/end-session"

When I see this, I will:
1. Generate a summary of what was done
2. Update ANCHOR.md, CLAUDE.md, IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md if anything changed
3. Create a log file: `logs/YYYY-MM-DD-N.md` with the summary
4. Sync context files to the Dropbox website folder
5. Commit project changes to GitHub with the summary
6. Commit website changes to its GitHub repo

---

## Part 3: Hook Configuration

For backup and timestamps, we'll configure hooks in `.claude/settings.json`:

```json
{
  "hooks": {
    "SessionStart": [
      {
        "matcher": "startup|resume",
        "hooks": [
          {
            "type": "command",
            "command": "echo \"[$(date '+%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S')] Session started\" >> ~/.claude/anchor-sessions.log"
          }
        ]
      }
    ],
    "SessionEnd": [
      {
        "hooks": [
          {
            "type": "command",
            "command": "echo \"[$(date '+%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S')] Session ended\" >> ~/.claude/anchor-sessions.log"
          }
        ]
      }
    ]
  }
}
```

The hooks are just for timestamps. The real work happens when you ask me to wrap up.

- Fixed Z

- Need to discuss X

`abc123` - "Implement project skeleton and main loop"

Here's how to connect a GitHub repository to Blot.im:

## Using Git with Blot.im and GitHub

Git supports multiple remote URLs, which means you can use the same repository on Blot and GitHub.

### If You Have an Existing GitHub Repository

If you have an existing repository on GitHub, you can add Blot's endpoint:

```bash
git remote set-url --add --push origin https://blot.im/your-repo.git
```

### If You Have an Existing Blot Repository

If you have an existing repository on Blot, you can add GitHub's endpoint:

```bash
git remote set-url --add --push origin https://github.com/your-repo.git
```

### After Adding the Remote

The next time you push, you will need to use the force flag to ensure the changes go through on both Blot and GitHub:

... [16 more lines]

    "text": "Based on my research of the official Claude Code documentation, here's comprehensive information about session persistence and recovery:\n\n## Summary of Claude Code Session Persistence & Recovery\n\n### 1. What Happens When Terminal Closes Unexpectedly\n\n**Current behavior:** When your terminal closes unexpectedly, your current Claude Code session is **interrupted**. However, Claude Code does NOT lose the complete session history and work\u2014it's preserved on disk and can be resumed later.\n\n**Important limitation:** Any real-time context that was loaded in memory (like file readings, search results, or active bash processes) will need to be re-established when you resume. The core conversation and file edits are preserved, but you'll need to rebuild immediate context.\n\n### 2. Automatic Session Saving/Persistence\n\n**Yes, sessions are automatically saved.** Here's how:\n\n- **Conversation Storage**: All conversations are automatically saved locally with their full message history\n- **Message Deserialization**: When resuming, the entire message history is restored to maintain context\n- **Tool State**: Tool usage and results from previous conversations are preserved\n- **Checkpoints**: Claude Code automatically tracks file edits, creating checkpoints before each edit:\n  - Every user prompt creates a new checkpoint\n  - **Checkpoints persist across sessions**, so you can access them in resumed conversations\n  - Automatically cleaned up after 30 days (configurable)\n\n### 3. Session Resumption Commands\n\n**Yes, sessions can be resumed after terminal closes.** Use these commands:\n\n**From command line:**\n\n```bash\n# Continue the most recent conversation in the current directory\nclaude --continue\n\n# Or use the shorthand\nclaude -c\n```\n\n```bash\n# Resume a specific session by ID or name\nclaude --resume \"session-name\"\nclaude -r \"auth-refactor\"\n```\n\n```bash\n# Open interactive session picker (if you don't remember the name)\nclaude --resume\n```\n\n**From within an active session:**\n\n```\n/resume          # Switch to a different conversation\n/rename          # Name the current session for easy recall later\n```\n\n**Session picker features** (when using `claude --resume`):\n\n| Shortcut | Action |\n|----------|--------|\n| \u2191 / \u2193 | Navigate between sessions |\n| Enter | Resume selected session |\n| P | Preview session content before resuming |\n| R | Rename the highlighted session |\n| / | Search to filter sessions |\n| B | Filter to sessions from current git branch |\n| Esc | Exit the picker |\n\n### 4. Where Session Transcripts Are Stored\n\n**Session location:** Sessions are stored **locally per project directory**. The exact storage location is managed by Claude Code internally and varies by system.\n\n**Key storage facts:**\n\n- Sessions are organized by git repository (including worktrees)\n- The `/resume` picker shows sessions from the same git repository\n- Sessions persist across system restarts (stored on disk, not in memory)\n- **Checkpoints associated with sessions** also persist to disk\n\n**Project-level memory files** (complementary to sessions):\n\n- **`CLAUDE.md`** or **`.claude/CLAUDE.md`** - Team-shared project instructions\n- **`CLAUDE.local.md`** - Personal project-specific preferences (auto-added to .gitignore, not shared)\n- **`~/.claude/CLAUDE.md`** - Personal preferences for all projects\n- **`.claude/rules/*.md`** - Modular, topic-specific project instructions\n\nThese memory files are automatically loaded when sessions start, providing persistent context across resume operations.\n\n### 5. Session Recovery Features\n\n**Checkpointing (Safety Net):**\n\n```\nPress: Esc + Esc  (or use /rewind command)\n```\n\nThis opens the rewind menu where you can restore:\n- **Conversation only** - Rewind to a user message while keeping code changes\n- **Code only** - Revert file changes while keeping the conversation\n- **Both** - Restore both to a prior point\n\nCheckpoints automatically track Claude's file edits before each turn, allowing you to undo unwanted changes.\n\n### 6. Best Practices for Session Management\n\n**Naming conventions:**\n```bash\n# Name sessions early with descriptive names\n/rename auth-refactor\n/rename payment-integration\n\n# Then resume by name later\nclaude --resume auth-refactor\n```\n\n**For long work sessions:**\n- Use `/checkpoint` or `/rewind` frequently to save known-good states\n- Create named sessions for distinct tasks to avoid confusion\n- Use CLAUDE.md for persistent instructions instead of repeating context\n\n**For team environments:**\n- Store project memory in `./CLAUDE.md` for team sharing\n- Use `.claude/rules/` for modular, topic-specific guidelines\n- All team members automatically get these memories when cloning the repo\n\n### 7. Known Limitations\n\n- **Bash command changes not tracked**: Changes made by bash commands (rm, mv, cp) cannot be undone through rewind\u2014only direct file edits through Claude's tools are tracked\n- **External changes not tracked**: Files modified outside Claude Code in concurrent sessions aren't captured unless they overlap with current session edits\n- **Checkpointing is session-level**: It's designed for quick recovery, not permanent history\u2014use Git for long-term version control\n\n---\n\n**Key Takeaway:** Claude Code sessions have robust automatic persistence. Your work is never lost when a terminal closes unexpectedly. Simply use `claude --continue` or `claude --resume` to pick up where you left off with full context restored.\n\nSources:\n- [Checkpointing - Claude Code Docs](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/checkpointing.md)\n- [Common Workflows - Resume Previous Conversations](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/common-workflows.md)\n- [Memory Management - Claude Code Docs](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/memory.md)\n- [CLI Reference - Claude Code Docs](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/cli-reference.md)\n- [Feature Request: Session Resumption for Claude Code CLI](https://github.com/anthropics/claude-code/issues/1340)\n- [How I Solved Claude Code's Context Loss Problem](https://dev.to/kaz123/how-i-solved-claude-codes-context-loss-problem-with-a-lightweight-session-manager-265d)"

```
# Setting Up a Blot Blog with GitHub Sync

Based on the repository documentation, here's how to synchronize a Blot blog with GitHub:

## Steps

1. **Clone your Blot repository locally** using the git URL provided by Blot when setting up your blog.

2. **Create an empty GitHub repository** and copy its URL.

3. **Add GitHub as a second remote** by running:
   ```
   git remote add github [your-github-url]
   ```

4. **Verify both remotes are configured** with `git remote -v` to confirm you have both the Blot origin and GitHub remote set up.

5. **Push to either remote as needed** using `git push github` for GitHub or `git push origin` for Blot.

## Optional Enhancement

The documentation suggests "following instructions to make `git push` automatically synchronize with both remotes," which would streamline the workflow by eliminating the need to specify which remote receives each push.

This approach allows you to maintain your Blot blog while keeping a backup or public copy on GitHub by managing multiple git remotes in a single local repository.
```

Great research results. Here's what I found:

---

## Session Persistence (Good News!)

**Sessions are NOT lost when you close the terminal.** Claude Code automatically saves:

- Full conversation history
- File edit checkpoints
- Tool usage and results

**To resume after closing:**
```bash
claude --continue    # or claude -c (continues most recent)
claude --resume      # opens a session picker
```

**Checkpoints**: Every time you submit a prompt, Claude Code creates a checkpoint. You can rewind with `Esc + Esc` or `/rewind`. Checkpoints persist across sessions for 30 days.

So if you accidentally close the terminal, just run `claude -c` and you're back.

---

## Blot GitHub Integration

Blot supports Git natively. You can push to **both Blot and GitHub** with a single `git push`:

### How It Works

1. **Blot gives you a Git URL** like `https://blot.im/git/username/blogname`
2. **You add GitHub as a second push target**:
   ```bash
   git remote set-url --add --push origin git@github.com:a327ex/anchor.git
   ```
3. **Now `git push` sends to both** Blot and GitHub simultaneously

### Your Setup Would Be:

```
a327ex/Anchor/           ← Main project + GitHub repo
├── website/             ← Blot website folder
│   ├── logs/
│   ├── pages/
│   ├── context/
│   └── ...
├── src/                 ← C source (future)
├── yue/                 ← YueScript (future)
├── CLAUDE.md
├── ANCHOR.md
└── IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md
```

**But there's a consideration**: Blot expects the blog content at the root of what it syncs. If your website is in a subfolder, you'd need to either:

1. **Separate repos**: `a327ex/anchor` for project, `a327ex/anchor-website` for Blot (simpler)
2. **Git subtree**: Push only the `website/` folder to Blot
3. **Blot folder setting**: Check if Blot can be configured to look at a subfolder

Let me check if Blot supports subfolder configuration...

Based on the search results, here's what I found about Blot.im and folder configuration:

## Blot.im Folder Configuration

Blot is an express.js node application that turns a folder into a blog.(no content)You can edit the folder for the example blog inside the `data` directory at `./data/blogs/blog_$ID`.

### Folder Structure Options

Blot's documentation covers: Sync the folder, Dropbox, Git, Google Drive, iCloud, Files, Markdown, Images, and importantly **Subfolders** as a topic.(no content)A recommended approach is to have each post in its self-contained folder with all images adjacent to the source file. This means you can embed images using Markdown with a relative path like `![Caption](_image.jpg)`.(no content)Posts can be automatically dated based on their path (e.g., `2021/02-06 Pears/post.txt`).

### Drafts and Publishing

For drafts, you can stick the post subfolder inside the Drafts folder, and move it outside when ready to publish - the relative paths will mean that any images won't break.

### Configuration Dashboard

Your site settings are configured on your dashboard, which includes options for: using your own domain, setting up redirects and preventing broken links, and changing the format of your post URLs.

The search results don't show specific documentation about configuring a subfolder as the blog root folder. You may want to check Blot's official documentation at **blot.im/how/configure** directly or contact **support@blot.im** for more specific configuration options regarding root folder settings.


REMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks.
```

Blot doesn't appear to have a built-in "root folder" setting. Here are your options:

---

## Option 1: Separate Repos (Simplest)

```
GitHub:
├── a327ex/anchor           ← Main project
└── a327ex/anchor-website   ← Blot website
```

- Clean separation
- Push project changes to `anchor`
- Push website changes to `anchor-website` (which also pushes to Blot)

---

## Option 2: Git Subtree (Single Repo)

Keep everything in one repo, but push only `website/` to Blot:

```bash
# Push only the website folder to Blot
git subtree push --prefix=website blot main
```

You'd need to add Blot as a separate remote:
```bash
git remote add blot https://blot.im/git/username/anchor
```

Then after commits:
```bash
git push origin main              # Push everything to GitHub
git subtree push --prefix=website blot main  # Push website to Blot
```

This could be automated in the end-of-session script.

---

## Option 3: Symlink in Dropbox (Fallback)

Keep project in `a327ex/Anchor`, symlink or copy `website/` to Dropbox at session end. Less elegant but works.

---

## My Recommendation

**Option 2 (Git Subtree)** gives you:
- One repo to manage
- Clean separation between code and website
- Single source of truth
- Automated with a script at session end

The end-of-session workflow would be:
1. Commit all changes to main repo
2. `git push origin main` (goes to GitHub)
3. `git subtree push --prefix=website blot main` (syncs website to Blot)

---

### Hook Configuration (`.claude/settings.json`)

```json
{
  "hooks": {
    "SessionStart": [
      {
        "matcher": "startup|resume",
        "hooks": [
          {
            "type": "command",
            "command": "echo \"[$(date '+%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S')] Session started\" >> /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/session-timestamps.log"
          }
        ]
      }
    ],
    "SessionEnd": [
      {
        "hooks": [
          {
            "type": "command", 
            "command": "echo \"[$(date '+%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S')] Session ended\" >> /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/session-timestamps.log"
          }
        ]
      }
    ]
  }
}
```

Purpose: Backup timestamps in case end-of-session ritual isn't run.

---

### Session Log Format

Each log in `website/logs/YYYY-MM-DD-N.md`:

```markdown
---
date: 2025-12-31
session: 1
---

# Session Log: 2025-12-31 #1

## Summary
Brief 2-3 sentence description of what was accomplished.

## Changes Made
- Bullet list of significant changes
- Updated X in ANCHOR.md
- Implemented Y feature
- Fixed Z bug

## Files Modified
- src/main.c
- yue/object.yue
- IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md

## Implementation Progress
Current phase and what's checked off.

## Notes for Next Session
- Continue with X
- Need to discuss Y
- Blocked on Z

## Commit
`abc1234` - "Implement project skeleton and main loop"
```

### End-of-Session Workflow (Manual)

When you say **"let's wrap up"** (or similar), I will:

| Step | Action |
|------|--------|
| 1 | Generate a summary of the session |
| 2 | Create `website/logs/YYYY-MM-DD-N.md` with the summary |
| 3 | Update `ANCHOR.md` if any APIs changed |
| 4 | Update `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` if tasks were completed |
| 5 | Update `CLAUDE.md` if new patterns/conventions were established |
| 6 | Copy updated docs to `website/context/` |
| 7 | `git add -A` |
| 8 | `git commit -m "<summary>"` |
| 9 | `git push origin main` |
| 10 | `git subtree push --prefix=website blot main` |
| 11 | Confirm everything pushed successfully |

No SSH keys yet. Let's set them up:

```bash
# Generate a new SSH key
ssh-keygen -t ed25519 -C "a327ex@gmail.com"

# Press Enter for default location (~/.ssh/id_ed25519)
# Optional: add a passphrase or press Enter for none
```

Then:
```bash
# Start the SSH agent
eval "$(ssh-agent -s)"

# Add your key
ssh-add ~/.ssh/id_ed25519

# Copy the public key
cat ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub
```

Finally, add the public key to GitHub:
1. Go to https://github.com/settings/keys
2. Click "New SSH key"
3. Paste the key and save

After that, verify with:
```bash
ssh -T git@github.com
```

Should say: `Hi a327ex! You've successfully authenticated...`

> What's ed25519?

**Ed25519** is a modern cryptographic algorithm for SSH keys. Compared to the older RSA:

| | Ed25519 | RSA |
|---|---------|-----|
| Key size | 256 bits | 2048-4096 bits |
| Security | Equivalent to RSA 3000-bit | Good at 4096-bit |
| Speed | Faster | Slower |
| Key length | Short (~68 chars) | Long (~400+ chars) |

It's the current recommended choice. GitHub, GitLab, and most services support it.

If you have compatibility concerns with older systems, use RSA instead:
```bash
ssh-keygen -t rsa -b 4096 -C "a327ex@gmail.com"
```

But for GitHub and modern use, ed25519 is preferred.

> ssh-keygen -t ed25519 -C "a327ex@gmail.com"

> Should I have a password for the folder? It's telling me to add one or leave it empty.

**No passphrase is fine** for personal machines. It means you won't have to type a password every time you push/pull.

**With passphrase** adds security if someone gets access to your machine, but you'd need to enter it on each use (or use ssh-agent to cache it).

For your home machine: **leave it empty** (just press Enter twice). Simpler workflow, and your machine is already password-protected.

> How do I copy the key to my clipboard?

```bash
cat ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub | wl-copy
```

Or just display it and select with mouse:
```bash
cat ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub
```

> Ok, it's working. The blot git repo is https://blot.im/clients/git/end/anchor.git, username adonaac@gmail.com and a specific passowrd, but I don't know if I should paste it here.

**Don't paste the password here** — I don't need it and it would be in the conversation history.

For Blot authentication, you have two options:

1. **Git credential helper** (stores password so you don't retype it):
   ```bash
   git config --global credential.helper store
   ```
   Next time you push to Blot, enter the password once and it'll be saved.

2. **Include in URL** (less secure, but works):
   ```bash
   git remote add blot https://adonaac%40gmail.com:PASSWORD@blot.im/clients/git/end/anchor.git
   ```
   (The `%40` is the encoded `@` in your email)

I recommend option 1. When we set up the repo, you'll enter the Blot password once on first push and it'll be cached.

> Oh, yea, I was gonna ask, should I run it from the Anchor directory for it to be able to read that CLAUDE.MD file? What if I want to use you for purposes other than just that project, like random conversations, but also have the start/end session thing going on?

**CLAUDE.md is directory-based.** Claude Code reads it from your current working directory (and walks up parent directories).

### How to organize:

| Purpose | Directory | What happens |
|---------|-----------|--------------|
| Anchor work | `~/a327ex/Anchor/` | Reads Anchor's CLAUDE.md, session workflow applies |
| Random chat | `~/` or anywhere else | No project CLAUDE.md, just casual use |

### Options for random conversations:

1. **Just run from home directory:**
   ```bash
   cd ~
   claude
   ```
   No CLAUDE.md there = no special workflow instructions.

2. **Global instructions** (optional): If you want some instructions everywhere, put them in:
   ```
   ~/.claude/CLAUDE.md
   ```
   This applies to all sessions regardless of directory.

3. **Skip the ritual**: Even in the Anchor directory, the end-of-session workflow only runs when you ask me to wrap up. For casual Anchor questions, just exit normally without the ritual.

### My recommendation:

- **Anchor work**: `cd ~/a327ex/Anchor && claude` (or `claude -c` to resume)
- **Random stuff**: `cd ~ && claude`

The session start/end hooks in `.claude/settings.json` will be project-specific (inside `~/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/`), so they won't affect other directories.

> OK, to continue this conversation then I don't need to run from the Anchor folder (it doesn't exist yet), because we haven't actually done the setup yet, right?

Correct. Just run `claude -c` from wherever you started this session (your home directory `~`). 

The Anchor setup doesn't exist yet, so there's nothing to read. Once you're back, we'll:

1. Create `~/a327ex/Anchor/`
2. Move the existing files there
3. Set up the structure
4. Initialize git

Then future Anchor sessions would start from that directory.

> If I run just claude by mistake, can I still recover the conversation?

Yes. Run:

```bash
claude --resume
```

It opens a session picker showing all previous sessions. Use arrow keys to select this one, press Enter to resume.

You can also search/filter in the picker with `/`.

Or if that needs sudo:

No problem, let's fix it:

```bash
pkill dropbox
mv ~/Dropbox ~/dropbox
ln -s ~/dropbox ~/Dropbox
dropbox &
```

This moves the folder to `dropbox` and creates a symlink so Dropbox still finds it.

No, this won't change anything on Dropbox's servers. We're just renaming the local folder and creating a symlink pointing to it - Dropbox will still see all files in the same place.

But yes, pausing or killing Dropbox first is safer to avoid any sync conflicts during the move. The `pkill dropbox` command stops it completely, so just run those commands in order and you're good.

Looks like it worked - `dropbox` exists. Let's check if Dropbox is using it correctly and clean up the old folder:

The symlink is already there. To make Dropbox use `dropbox` directly:

This updates Dropbox's config to point to `dropbox` directly and removes the symlink.

No worries - `~/Dropbox` is just a symlink (a pointer) to `~/dropbox`. Your actual files are in `~/dropbox`.

Running `rm ~/Dropbox` only removes the symlink, not the folder it points to. Your files in `~/dropbox` stay untouched, and nothing changes on the server.

Click **"Relink"** - it will reconnect to your account using the new `dropbox` path we set in the config. Your files are still there and it will continue syncing normally.

1→# Anchor Implementation Plan
     2→
     3→C-first approach with YueScript as the scripting layer. Minimize plain Lua; write game-facing code in YueScript from the start.
     4→

     6→

     8→
     9→## Build Strategy
    10→
    11→### YueScript Compilation
    12→
    13→**Decision: Build-time compilation**
    14→
    15→Compile `.yue` → `.lua` during build, not at runtime. This:
    16→- Eliminates runtime dependency on YueScript compiler
    17→- Produces debuggable `.lua` files for error inspection
    18→- Simplifies the C side (just loads standard Lua)
    19→- Can add runtime compilation later for hot reloading
    20→
    21→**Build flow:**
    22→```
    23→game.yue ──► yue -r ──► game.lua ──► embedded in executable / loaded at runtime
    24→                ↑
    25→                └── -r flag preserves line numbers for debugging

---

## Summary

**The Project:** You're building **Anchor**, a game engine in C with YueScript/Lua scripting, and using it to make **Emoji Ball Battles** — a physics auto-battler roguelite with Minesweeper-style dungeon navigation.

### What Anchor Is

A minimalist 2D engine with:
- **C backend**: SDL2 for window/input/audio, software rendering, Box2D 3.1 for physics
- **YueScript frontend**: All game logic in YueScript (MoonScript variant) compiling to Lua
- **Object tree**: Parent-child hierarchy where children die with parents, automatic cleanup
- **Operator syntax**: Concise DSL (`E()`, `^`, `/`, `+`, `>>`) for object construction
- **Springs/Timers**: Built-in "juice" primitives for game feel

### The Game: Emoji Ball Battles

- **Dungeon phase**: Navigate a Minesweeper grid, avoid mines, find the boss
- **Combat phase**: Physics-based auto-battler where emoji balls collide and damage each other
- **Roguelite loop**: Survive floors, collect upgrades, grow stronger

### How We'll Work

1. **Incremental steps** — small pieces, show/test, get feedback, then proceed
2. **No autonomous juice** — you have specific taste; I ask or leave mechanical
3. **C for low-level, YueScript for logic** — only move to C when profiling shows need
4. **Locality** — code should be readable in one place, no scattered systems
5. **Documentation** — keep ANCHOR.md and CLAUDE.md updated as we implement

### Current State

The engine is **not yet implemented**. The .yue files are example game code showing how the API *will* work once we build the C foundation. The implementation plan has 8 phases:

1. C Skeleton + Web Build
2. Rendering (software, layers, effects)
3. Input
4. Audio
5. Physics (Box2D 3.1)
6. Random
6.5. Text Rendering
7. YueScript Object System
8. Emoji Ball Battles (the actual game)

---

Ready to start building when you are.

One test I run with AI models to see if they're good is trying to get them to code like me. I upload a bunch of prototypes I wrote in the past, give them my engine's documentation, and tell them to try to code something new.

In the past, they all failed. I couldn't tell if it was because of poor reasoning skills, or too small context, but they just couldn't do it. This changed last month (November 2025) with the release of Opus 4.5.

This is the first time a model has been able to succeed at this task, and now I have the computer coding exactly like I do, not only using my engine's API correctly, but also following my coding style and my coding *philosophy,* which has some fairly high-level "soft" concepts to it.

This is, functionally, my "AGI is here" moment. As I was finishing [It Follows](https://a327ex.com/posts/it_follows) during December, I spent maybe 3-4 days in total talking to Opus to assess its capabilities. I was pleasantly surprised. It understands your intent, catches its own mistakes, and has genuine taste.

Other models can do some of this, but something always feels off, like they're too literal, or subtly bullshitting you. Opus instead is... *coherent?* Everything works together and it feels like talking to someone who actually understands, it just thinks correctly.

And so my conversations with it generated these documents (view them with formatting on the sidebar to the left):

[Anchor Engine Specification](/files/Opus 4.5/ANCHOR.md)  
[CLAUDE.md](/files/Opus 4.5/CLAUDE.md)  
[Anchor Implementation Plan](/files/Opus 4.5/IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md)  

This is the specification of my engine and how Claude should work with it, but also a plan to implement it in C, which is something I've been meaning to do for a long time to move away from LÖVE, but never got around to.

We also made quite a few improvements to the engine's API that I likely wouldn't have been able to do by myself. My favorite was telling it to read [Amulet's docs](https://www.amulet.xyz/doc/) and asking how we could steal the operator syntax it uses for its scene graph.

It did so and proposed the operator syntax I ended up using for inline anonymous object creation, with symbols such as `^`, `/`, `>>`:

```yuescript
spawn_particle = (x, y, color, vx, vy) ->
  E! ^ {x: x, y: y, color: color, radius: 3}
     ^ =>
... [23 more lines]
```

Excellent, now I have a complete picture. Let me lay out a detailed plan:

---

## Detailed Plan for Session Workflow

### The Challenge

Claude Code hooks run shell commands *after* I'm done responding, but at that point I can't generate new content. So we need a **two-part approach**:

1. **Manual end-of-session ritual** — You tell me to wrap up, I do all the work
2. **Hooks** — For logging timestamps and ensuring nothing is missed

---

## Part 1: File Structure

### Project Directory (`/home/adn/a327ex/`)

```
a327ex/
├── CLAUDE.md              ← Instructions for ALL Claude instances
├── ANCHOR.md              ← Engine spec (I update this)
├── IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md ← Checklist (I update this)
├── .claude/
│   ├── settings.json      ← Hook configuration
│   └── hooks/
│       └── session-end.sh ← Backup logging script
├── src/                   ← (future) C source
├── yue/                   ← (future) YueScript source
└── ...
```

### Dropbox Website (`/home/adn/dropbox/Apps/Blot/Anchor/`)

```
Anchor/
├── context/               ← Copies of ANCHOR.md, CLAUDE.md, etc. (for website)
├── logs/                  ← Session logs (YYYY-MM-DD-N.md)
├── pages/                 ← Website pages
├── files/                 ← Downloadable files
└── notes/                 ← Notes
```

### GitHub Repos (to create)

1. **`a327ex/anchor`** — Main project repo
2. **`a327ex/anchor-website`** — Blot website repo (or could be same repo, different branch)

---

## Part 2: The CLAUDE.md Workflow Section

I'll add a section to CLAUDE.md that every future Claude instance will read. It will instruct them to:

1. **At session start**: Review what's pending in IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md
2. **During session**: Update docs as APIs/plans change
3. **At session end**: Run the end-of-session ritual when asked

The key is a **specific command** you can say:

> "Let's wrap up this session" or "/end-session"

When I see this, I will:
1. Generate a summary of what was done
2. Update ANCHOR.md, CLAUDE.md, IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md if anything changed
3. Create a log file: `logs/YYYY-MM-DD-N.md` with the summary
4. Sync context files to the Dropbox website folder
5. Commit project changes to GitHub with the summary
6. Commit website changes to its GitHub repo

---

## Part 3: Hook Configuration

For backup and timestamps, we'll configure hooks in `.claude/settings.json`:

```json
{
  "hooks": {
    "SessionStart": [
      {
        "matcher": "startup|resume",
        "hooks": [
          {
            "type": "command",
            "command": "echo \"[$(date '+%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S')] Session started\" >> ~/.claude/anchor-sessions.log"
          }
        ]
      }
    ],
    "SessionEnd": [
      {
        "hooks": [
          {
            "type": "command",
            "command": "echo \"[$(date '+%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S')] Session ended\" >> ~/.claude/anchor-sessions.log"
          }
        ]
      }
    ]
  }
}
```

The hooks are just for timestamps. The real work happens when you ask me to wrap up.

---

Here's what we need to do now:

### Step 1: Initialize Git repos
```bash
# Project repo
cd /home/adn/a327ex

cd /home/adn/dropbox/Apps/Blot/Anchor

git remote add origin git@github.com:a327ex/anchor-website.git
```

### Step 2: Update CLAUDE.md with workflow instructions
Add a section that future Claude instances will read and follow.

### Step 3: Create the hook configuration
Set up `.claude/settings.json` in the project.

### Step 4: Create a session log template
Standardize the format for logs.

### Step 5: Sync context files
Ensure ANCHOR.md, CLAUDE.md, IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md are mirrored to the Dropbox website.

---

## Part 5: Session Log Format

Each log will be a Markdown file like `2025-12-31-1.md`:

```markdown
# Session Log: 2025-12-31 #1

## Summary
Brief description of what was accomplished.

## Changes Made
- Updated X in ANCHOR.md
- Implemented Y
- Fixed Z

## Files Modified
- src/main.c
- yue/object.yue

## Implementation Progress
- [x] Phase 1.1: Project setup
- [ ] Phase 1.2: Main loop (in progress)

## Notes for Next Session
- Continue with main loop
- Need to discuss X

## Commit
`abc123` - "Implement project skeleton and main loop"
```

---

## Questions Before Proceeding

1. **GitHub repos**: Do you already have `a327ex/anchor` on GitHub, or should I help create it? Same for the website repo.

2. **Repo structure**: Should the website be a separate repo, or a branch/subfolder of the main repo?

3. **Sync strategy**: Should the context files (ANCHOR.md, etc.) be:
   - **Copied** from a327ex → Dropbox on each session end, or
   - **Symlinked** so they're always in sync, or
   - **The canonical source** be in Dropbox and a327ex just links to it?

4. **Log format**: Is the format I proposed above good, or do you want changes?

5. **Trigger phrase**: What should you say to trigger the end-of-session ritual? Options:
   - "Let's wrap up this session"
   - "/end-session" (feels like a command)
   - "Session end"
   - Something else?

Once you answer these, I'll implement everything.

Here's how to connect a GitHub repository to Blot.im:

## Using Git with Blot.im and GitHub

Git supports multiple remote URLs, which means you can use the same repository on Blot and GitHub.

### If You Have an Existing GitHub Repository

If you have an existing repository on GitHub, you can add Blot's endpoint:

```bash
git remote set-url --add --push origin https://blot.im/your-repo.git
```

### If You Have an Existing Blot Repository

If you have an existing repository on Blot, you can add GitHub's endpoint:

```bash
git remote set-url --add --push origin https://github.com/your-repo.git
```

### After Adding the Remote

The next time you push, you will need to use the force flag to ensure the changes go through on both Blot and GitHub:

... [16 more lines]

[
  {

    "text": "Based on my research of the official Claude Code documentation, here's comprehensive information about session persistence and recovery:\n\n## Summary of Claude Code Session Persistence & Recovery\n\n### 1. What Happens When Terminal Closes Unexpectedly\n\n**Current behavior:** When your terminal closes unexpectedly, your current Claude Code session is **interrupted**. However, Claude Code does NOT lose the complete session history and work\u2014it's preserved on disk and can be resumed later.\n\n**Important limitation:** Any real-time context that was loaded in memory (like file readings, search results, or active bash processes) will need to be re-established when you resume. The core conversation and file edits are preserved, but you'll need to rebuild immediate context.\n\n### 2. Automatic Session Saving/Persistence\n\n**Yes, sessions are automatically saved.** Here's how:\n\n- **Conversation Storage**: All conversations are automatically saved locally with their full message history\n- **Message Deserialization**: When resuming, the entire message history is restored to maintain context\n- **Tool State**: Tool usage and results from previous conversations are preserved\n- **Checkpoints**: Claude Code automatically tracks file edits, creating checkpoints before each edit:\n  - Every user prompt creates a new checkpoint\n  - **Checkpoints persist across sessions**, so you can access them in resumed conversations\n  - Automatically cleaned up after 30 days (configurable)\n\n### 3. Session Resumption Commands\n\n**Yes, sessions can be resumed after terminal closes.** Use these commands:\n\n**From command line:**\n\n```bash\n# Continue the most recent conversation in the current directory\nclaude --continue\n\n# Or use the shorthand\nclaude -c\n```\n\n```bash\n# Resume a specific session by ID or name\nclaude --resume \"session-name\"\nclaude -r \"auth-refactor\"\n```\n\n```bash\n# Open interactive session picker (if you don't remember the name)\nclaude --resume\n```\n\n**From within an active session:**\n\n```\n/resume          # Switch to a different conversation\n/rename          # Name the current session for easy recall later\n```\n\n**Session picker features** (when using `claude --resume`):\n\n| Shortcut | Action |\n|----------|--------|\n| \u2191 / \u2193 | Navigate between sessions |\n| Enter | Resume selected session |\n| P | Preview session content before resuming |\n| R | Rename the highlighted session |\n| / | Search to filter sessions |\n| B | Filter to sessions from current git branch |\n| Esc | Exit the picker |\n\n### 4. Where Session Transcripts Are Stored\n\n**Session location:** Sessions are stored **locally per project directory**. The exact storage location is managed by Claude Code internally and varies by system.\n\n**Key storage facts:**\n\n- Sessions are organized by git repository (including worktrees)\n- The `/resume` picker shows sessions from the same git repository\n- Sessions persist across system restarts (stored on disk, not in memory)\n- **Checkpoints associated with sessions** also persist to disk\n\n**Project-level memory files** (complementary to sessions):\n\n- **`CLAUDE.md`** or **`.claude/CLAUDE.md`** - Team-shared project instructions\n- **`CLAUDE.local.md`** - Personal project-specific preferences (auto-added to .gitignore, not shared)\n- **`~/.claude/CLAUDE.md`** - Personal preferences for all projects\n- **`.claude/rules/*.md`** - Modular, topic-specific project instructions\n\nThese memory files are automatically loaded when sessions start, providing persistent context across resume operations.\n\n### 5. Session Recovery Features\n\n**Checkpointing (Safety Net):**\n\n```\nPress: Esc + Esc  (or use /rewind command)\n```\n\nThis opens the rewind menu where you can restore:\n- **Conversation only** - Rewind to a user message while keeping code changes\n- **Code only** - Revert file changes while keeping the conversation\n- **Both** - Restore both to a prior point\n\nCheckpoints automatically track Claude's file edits before each turn, allowing you to undo unwanted changes.\n\n### 6. Best Practices for Session Management\n\n**Naming conventions:**\n```bash\n# Name sessions early with descriptive names\n/rename auth-refactor\n/rename payment-integration\n\n# Then resume by name later\nclaude --resume auth-refactor\n```\n\n**For long work sessions:**\n- Use `/checkpoint` or `/rewind` frequently to save known-good states\n- Create named sessions for distinct tasks to avoid confusion\n- Use CLAUDE.md for persistent instructions instead of repeating context\n\n**For team environments:**\n- Store project memory in `./CLAUDE.md` for team sharing\n- Use `.claude/rules/` for modular, topic-specific guidelines\n- All team members automatically get these memories when cloning the repo\n\n### 7. Known Limitations\n\n- **Bash command changes not tracked**: Changes made by bash commands (rm, mv, cp) cannot be undone through rewind\u2014only direct file edits through Claude's tools are tracked\n- **External changes not tracked**: Files modified outside Claude Code in concurrent sessions aren't captured unless they overlap with current session edits\n- **Checkpointing is session-level**: It's designed for quick recovery, not permanent history\u2014use Git for long-term version control\n\n---\n\n**Key Takeaway:** Claude Code sessions have robust automatic persistence. Your work is never lost when a terminal closes unexpectedly. Simply use `claude --continue` or `claude --resume` to pick up where you left off with full context restored.\n\nSources:\n- [Checkpointing - Claude Code Docs](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/checkpointing.md)\n- [Common Workflows - Resume Previous Conversations](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/common-workflows.md)\n- [Memory Management - Claude Code Docs](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/memory.md)\n- [CLI Reference - Claude Code Docs](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/cli-reference.md)\n- [Feature Request: Session Resumption for Claude Code CLI](https://github.com/anthropics/claude-code/issues/1340)\n- [How I Solved Claude Code's Context Loss Problem](https://dev.to/kaz123/how-i-solved-claude-codes-context-loss-problem-with-a-lightweight-session-manager-265d)"

# Setting Up a Blot Blog with GitHub Sync

Based on the repository documentation, here's how to synchronize a Blot blog with GitHub:

## Steps

1. **Clone your Blot repository locally** using the git URL provided by Blot when setting up your blog.

2. **Create an empty GitHub repository** and copy its URL.

3. **Add GitHub as a second remote** by running:
   ```
   git remote add github [your-github-url]
   ```

4. **Verify both remotes are configured** with `git remote -v` to confirm you have both the Blot origin and GitHub remote set up.

5. **Push to either remote as needed** using `git push github` for GitHub or `git push origin` for Blot.

## Optional Enhancement

The documentation suggests "following instructions to make `git push` automatically synchronize with both remotes," which would streamline the workflow by eliminating the need to specify which remote receives each push.

This approach allows you to maintain your Blot blog while keeping a backup or public copy on GitHub by managing multiple git remotes in a single local repository.
```

Great research results. Here's what I found:

---

## Session Persistence (Good News!)

**Sessions are NOT lost when you close the terminal.** Claude Code automatically saves:

- Full conversation history
- File edit checkpoints
- Tool usage and results

**To resume after closing:**
```bash
claude --continue    # or claude -c (continues most recent)
claude --resume      # opens a session picker
```

**Checkpoints**: Every time you submit a prompt, Claude Code creates a checkpoint. You can rewind with `Esc + Esc` or `/rewind`. Checkpoints persist across sessions for 30 days.

So if you accidentally close the terminal, just run `claude -c` and you're back.

---

## Blot GitHub Integration

Blot supports Git natively. You can push to **both Blot and GitHub** with a single `git push`:

### How It Works

1. **Blot gives you a Git URL** like `https://blot.im/git/username/blogname`
2. **You add GitHub as a second push target**:
   ```bash
   git remote set-url --add --push origin git@github.com:a327ex/anchor.git
   ```
3. **Now `git push` sends to both** Blot and GitHub simultaneously

### Your Setup Would Be:

```
a327ex/Anchor/           ← Main project + GitHub repo
├── website/             ← Blot website folder
│   ├── logs/
│   ├── pages/
│   ├── context/
│   └── ...
├── src/                 ← C source (future)
├── yue/                 ← YueScript (future)
├── CLAUDE.md
├── ANCHOR.md
└── IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md
```

**But there's a consideration**: Blot expects the blog content at the root of what it syncs. If your website is in a subfolder, you'd need to either:

1. **Separate repos**: `a327ex/anchor` for project, `a327ex/anchor-website` for Blot (simpler)
2. **Git subtree**: Push only the `website/` folder to Blot
3. **Blot folder setting**: Check if Blot can be configured to look at a subfolder

Based on the search results, here's what I found about Blot.im and folder configuration:

## Blot.im Folder Configuration

Blot is an express.js node application that turns a folder into a blog.(no content)You can edit the folder for the example blog inside the `data` directory at `./data/blogs/blog_$ID`.

### Folder Structure Options

Blot's documentation covers: Sync the folder, Dropbox, Git, Google Drive, iCloud, Files, Markdown, Images, and importantly **Subfolders** as a topic.(no content)A recommended approach is to have each post in its self-contained folder with all images adjacent to the source file. This means you can embed images using Markdown with a relative path like `![Caption](_image.jpg)`.(no content)Posts can be automatically dated based on their path (e.g., `2021/02-06 Pears/post.txt`).

### Drafts and Publishing

For drafts, you can stick the post subfolder inside the Drafts folder, and move it outside when ready to publish - the relative paths will mean that any images won't break.

### Configuration Dashboard

Your site settings are configured on your dashboard, which includes options for: using your own domain, setting up redirects and preventing broken links, and changing the format of your post URLs.

The search results don't show specific documentation about configuring a subfolder as the blog root folder. You may want to check Blot's official documentation at **blot.im/how/configure** directly or contact **support@blot.im** for more specific configuration options regarding root folder settings.


REMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks.

Blot doesn't appear to have a built-in "root folder" setting. Here are your options:

---

## Option 1: Separate Repos (Simplest)

```
GitHub:
├── a327ex/anchor           ← Main project
└── a327ex/anchor-website   ← Blot website
```

- Clean separation
- Push project changes to `anchor`
- Push website changes to `anchor-website` (which also pushes to Blot)

---

## Option 2: Git Subtree (Single Repo)

Keep everything in one repo, but push only `website/` to Blot:

```bash
# Push only the website folder to Blot
git subtree push --prefix=website blot main
```

You'd need to add Blot as a separate remote:
```bash
git remote add blot https://blot.im/git/username/anchor
```

Then after commits:
```bash
git push origin main              # Push everything to GitHub
git subtree push --prefix=website blot main  # Push website to Blot
```

This could be automated in the end-of-session script.

---

## Option 3: Symlink in Dropbox (Fallback)

Keep project in `a327ex/Anchor`, symlink or copy `website/` to Dropbox at session end. Less elegant but works.

---

## My Recommendation

**Option 2 (Git Subtree)** gives you:
- One repo to manage
- Clean separation between code and website
- Single source of truth
- Automated with a script at session end

The end-of-session workflow would be:
1. Commit all changes to main repo
2. `git push origin main` (goes to GitHub)
3. `git subtree push --prefix=website blot main` (syncs website to Blot)

          }
        ]
      }
    ],

      {
        "hooks": [
          {
            "type": "command",

          }
        ]
      }
    ]
  }
}

```

⏺ Bash (ls -la ~/.ssh/ 2>/dev/null)

```
total 4
drwx------ 1 adn adn  22 Dec 31 14:59 .
drwx------ 1 adn adn 444 Dec 31 18:42 ..
-rw-r--r-- 1 adn adn  92 Dec 31 14:59 known_hosts
```

No SSH keys yet. Let's set them up:

```bash
# Generate a new SSH key
ssh-keygen -t ed25519 -C "a327ex@gmail.com"

# Press Enter for default location (~/.ssh/id_ed25519)
# Optional: add a passphrase or press Enter for none
```

Then:
```bash
# Start the SSH agent
eval "$(ssh-agent -s)"

# Add your key
ssh-add ~/.ssh/id_ed25519

# Copy the public key
cat ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub
```

Finally, add the public key to GitHub:
1. Go to https://github.com/settings/keys
2. Click "New SSH key"
3. Paste the key and save

After that, verify with:
```bash
ssh -T git@github.com
```

Should say: `Hi a327ex! You've successfully authenticated...`

**Ed25519** is a modern cryptographic algorithm for SSH keys. Compared to the older RSA:

| | Ed25519 | RSA |
|---|---------|-----|
| Key size | 256 bits | 2048-4096 bits |
| Security | Equivalent to RSA 3000-bit | Good at 4096-bit |
| Speed | Faster | Slower |
| Key length | Short (~68 chars) | Long (~400+ chars) |

It's the current recommended choice. GitHub, GitLab, and most services support it.

If you have compatibility concerns with older systems, use RSA instead:
```bash
ssh-keygen -t rsa -b 4096 -C "a327ex@gmail.com"
```

But for GitHub and modern use, ed25519 is preferred.

**No passphrase is fine** for personal machines. It means you won't have to type a password every time you push/pull.

**With passphrase** adds security if someone gets access to your machine, but you'd need to enter it on each use (or use ssh-agent to cache it).

For your home machine: **leave it empty** (just press Enter twice). Simpler workflow, and your machine is already password-protected.

```bash
cat ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub | wl-copy
```

Or just display it and select with mouse:
```bash
cat ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub
```

**Don't paste the password here** — I don't need it and it would be in the conversation history.

For Blot authentication, you have two options:

1. **Git credential helper** (stores password so you don't retype it):
   ```bash
   git config --global credential.helper store
   ```
   Next time you push to Blot, enter the password once and it'll be saved.

2. **Include in URL** (less secure, but works):
   ```bash
   git remote add blot https://adonaac%40gmail.com:PASSWORD@blot.im/clients/git/end/anchor.git
   ```
   (The `%40` is the encoded `@` in your email)

I recommend option 1. When we set up the repo, you'll enter the Blot password once on first push and it'll be cached.

- **Blot**: HTTPS with credentials (`https://blot.im/clients/git/end/anchor.git`)

```

> How can I tell when you're about to compact the conversation?

Claude Code shows a context usage indicator in the interface. You can see it in the bottom status area - it shows how much of the context window has been used (like a percentage or bar).

When context gets high (around 80-90%), you'll want to be mindful that compaction may happen soon. There's also usually a warning before automatic summarization occurs.

A few ways to manage this proactively:

1. **Check context usage** - Look at the status indicator periodically
2. **Manual compact** - You can type `/compact` to trigger summarization yourself at a natural breakpoint, which gives you more control over when it happens
3. **Resume sessions** - If context runs out mid-task, use `claude -c` or `claude --resume` to continue with the summarized context

For the Anchor project specifically, this is partly why we planned to have `CLAUDE.md` with comprehensive project context - even after compaction, a new or resumed session can read that file to get back up to speed quickly.

Would you like me to read the context files in `~/a327ex/` now so we have the full Anchor details fresh before proceeding with setup?

⏺ Task (Check context usage indicator)  
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;⎿ Result:

```
[
  {
    "type": "text",
    "text": "Based on my review of the Claude Code documentation, here's what I found regarding context usage indicators and warnings:\n\n## Current State: Limited Built-in Indicators\n\nClaude Code **does not currently provide a percentage bar, visual indicator, or explicit warning message** when context is about to run low or be compacted. The documentation doesn't mention any UI elements like progress bars or context usage percentages displayed to users.\n\n## How Context Management Works\n\nWhat Claude Code does provide:\n\n1. **Auto-compact behavior** (automatic):\n   - Claude automatically runs a compact operation when context exceeds **95% capacity**\n   - This is enabled by default but can be toggled via `/config` > \"Auto-compact enabled\"\n\n2. **Manual compaction** (`/compact` command):\n   - Users can manually trigger compaction when they notice the conversation is getting large\n   - You can add custom instructions: `/compact Focus on code samples and API usage`\n\n3. **PreCompact hook** (for advanced use):\n   - There's a `PreCompact` hook event that runs before compaction occurs\n   - This hook can detect whether compaction was triggered manually (`manual`) or automatically (`auto`)\n   - You could theoretically create a custom hook to provide warnings, but this would be user-implemented, not built-in\n\n## The Documentation Gap\n\nThe costs documentation mentions:\n> \"Claude uses auto-compact by default when context exceeds 95% capacity\"\n\nHowever, there's **no documentation about user-facing indicators** showing:\n- Current context usage percentage\n- Warnings before reaching 95%\n- Visual feedback during compaction\n\n## What You Can Do\n\nIf you need context usage indicators, you have a few options:\n\n1. **Use the `/cost` command** - This shows token usage statistics, which can give you a sense of conversation size, though it's not a direct context usage indicator\n\n2. **Create a custom PreCompact hook** - You could build a hook that notifies you when auto-compact is about to run. This would require implementing your own notification logic.\n\n3. **Request a feature** - Since this isn't currently documented as a built-in feature, you could use `/feedback` to request percentage indicators or context warnings as a feature enhancement.\n\nThis appears to be a gap in the current Claude Code UI/UX for context management visibility. Most users likely don't get explicit warnings and only realize compaction happened by noticing the conversation was reset to summaries."
  },
  {
    "type": "text",
    "text": "agentId: aaf5993 (for resuming to continue this agent's work if needed)"
  }
]
```

I was wrong about the percentage bar. Claude Code doesn't have a visible context usage indicator.

What actually happens:

- **Auto-compact at 95%** - Claude automatically compacts when context exceeds 95% capacity. This is on by default.
- **No warning** - There's no visual warning before it happens
- **`/compact`** - You can manually trigger compaction anytime with this command
- **`/cost`** - Shows token usage stats, which gives a rough sense of conversation size

So in practice, you won't know it's coming until it happens. The best approach for the Anchor project is what we planned: keep `CLAUDE.md` comprehensive so any session (new or post-compaction) can quickly get full context by reading it.

You can also run `/compact` yourself at natural breakpoints if you prefer to control when it happens rather than having it trigger automatically mid-task.

Claude Code stores all conversations on your machine, not in the cloud (for local usage). This means:\n\n- All conversation transcripts are saved to disk automatically\n- Sessions persist across terminal restarts\n- You can resume previous sessions even after unexpected terminal closures\n\n## Session Storage\n\nSessions are stored locally at:\n- **Location**: `~/.claude/projects/` (in your user directory)\n- **Format**: Session data is stored as conversation JSON files\n- **Retention**: By default, sessions are automatically cleaned up after **30 days** (this is configurable)\n\nThe key advantage: Since sessions are stored locally on your machine, an unexpected terminal closure doesn't result in lost session data.\n\n## Resuming After a Crash\n\nYou have several ways to resume work:\n\n```bash\n# Resume the most recent conversation\nclaude --continue\n\n# Open an interactive session picker\nclaude --resume\n\n# Resume a specific session by name\nclaude --resume session-name\n```\n\nFrom within an active session:\n```\n> /resume\n```\n\n## How Session Resumption Works\n\nAccording to the documentation, when you resume a session:\n\n1. **Full context restored**: The entire message history is deserialized and restored\n2. **Tool state preserved**: All tool usage and results from the previous conversation are maintained\n3. **Checkpoints available**: Since checkpoints persist across sessions, you can rewind to any previous state\n\n## Session Logs and History\n\n**Session logs would NOT be lost** if the terminal closes mid-session because:\n\n- Claude Code writes session data to disk continuously during the session\n- The transcript is automatically saved (you can find it at the `transcript_path` shown in hook inputs)\n- Even if Claude is in the middle of responding, your conversation history up to that point is preserved\n\n## Checkpoint System for Safety\n\nIn addition to session persistence, Claude Code has an automatic **checkpoint system**:\n\n- **Every user prompt creates a new checkpoint** automatically\n- Checkpoints persist across sessions, so you can access them in resumed conversations\n- Use `Esc` twice or `/rewind` to view and restore previous states\n\nThis provides a safety net: even if you accidentally close the terminal during a risky operation, you can rewind to a prior code state when you resume.\n\n## Data Retention by Account Type\n\n**Consumer users** (Free, Pro, Max):\n- If you allow data use for model improvement: 5-year retention\n- If you don't allow data use: 30-day retention\n\n**Commercial users** (Team, Enterprise, API):\n- Standard: 30-day retention\n- Can configure zero data retention or local caching (up to 30 days)\n\n## Best Practices for Session Management\n\n1. **Name your sessions** using `/rename` to find them later:\n   ```\n   > /rename auth-refactor\n   ```\n\n2. **Use descriptive names** early - makes resuming easier than generic names like \"explain this function\"\n\n3. **Trust the automatic save** - Claude Code continuously saves your conversation, so unexpected terminal closures won't lose data\n\n4. **Use checkpoints for code safety** - If Claude makes unwanted changes, use `/rewind` to recover previous code states\n\nThe bottom line: **Your session logs and conversation history are safe even if the terminal closes unexpectedly**. Claude Code automatically saves everything locally, and you can resume your work immediately using the resume commands.\n\nSources:\n- [Checkpointing Documentation](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/checkpointing.md)\n- [Common Workflows - Resume Previous Conversations](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/common-workflows.md#resume-previous-conversations)\n- [Data Usage and Retention Policies](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/data-usage.md)\n- [Hooks Reference - SessionStart and SessionEnd](https://code.claude.com/docs/en/hooks.md#sessionstart)"

Based on the search results, here's what I found about Blot.im's GitHub integration and subdirectory syncing:

## Blot.im Git Integration

Blot is a site generator that turns a folder into a website.

### Git Sync Setup

You can set up Blot to sync with GitHub by configuring multiple remotes: `git remote set-url --add --push origin https://blot.im/your-repo.git` or if you have an existing repository on Blot, you can add GitHub's endpoint.(no content)The next time you push, you will need to use the force flag to ensure the changes go through on both Blot and GitHub: `git push --force`.

### File Filtering

Blot automatically filters system files, temporary files, and other items that shouldn't be synced to keep your site clean. This includes hidden files and folders, version control directories, editor backup files, and operating system artifacts.

### Subfolders Documentation

Blot's documentation covers topics including Subfolders, Drafts, and Pages as part of their file organization features.(no content)The documentation notes that "the git client uses the master branch and repos from github will use main."

### No Direct Subdirectory Sync Feature Found

The search results don't show a specific feature in Blot.im that allows syncing only a subdirectory of a GitHub repository. The integration appears to sync the entire repository/folder rather than allowing you to specify a specific subfolder from a larger GitHub repo.

If you need to sync only a subdirectory from GitHub to Blot, you may need to use workarounds like git-subtree, sparse checkout, or maintaining a separate repository for just your Blot content.

# Blot Functionality Summary

Based on the repository information provided, I can offer limited insight into Blot's mechanics:

**What Blot Does:**
According to the README, "Blot turns a folder into a website." The project describes itself as a site generator that lets users "use your favorite tools to create whatever you publish."

**GitHub Sync Capabilities:**
Unfortunately, the provided content doesn't specify whether Blot supports syncing from GitHub repository subdirectories or requires the entire repo as the blog folder. The README focuses on local setup instructions using Docker, mkcert, and dnsmasq, but doesn't detail GitHub integration specifics.

**Architecture Overview:**
The README indicates the system uses "The internet <> Reverse proxy (Openresty) <> Blot (express.js node application) <> Redis" as its infrastructure.

For detailed information about GitHub synchronization options and subdirectory support, you would need to consult Blot's full documentation at blot.im or contact support@blot.im directly.
